        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2826584054" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8471209014853123420">2.75 stars rounded up to 3.0 <br/><br/>This 2011 novel has been on my list for years and after finding it at a second hand bookstore, I was excited to finally have my chance to experience this intriguing title. Although I <b> don't regret reading this in my free time, I am not exactly sure what the fuss was/is all about. </b> <br/><br/><i> I tend to describe this book as a love story between a brother and a sister-a story of how secrets are forged in childhood and are carried through to adulthood.( Sarah Winman, Author's Not</i></span>
  <span id="freeText8471209014853123420" style="display:none">2.75 stars rounded up to 3.0 <br /><br />This 2011 novel has been on my list for years and after finding it at a second hand bookstore, I was excited to finally have my chance to experience this intriguing title. Although I <b> don't regret reading this in my free time, I am not exactly sure what the fuss was/is all about. </b> <br /><br /><i> I tend to describe this book as a love story between a brother and a sister-a story of how secrets are forged in childhood and are carried through to adulthood.( Sarah Winman, Author's Note pg. 329) </i> <br /><br />I feel the need to lean on the author's intentions in my review. The relationship between Elly and Joe certainly was a special one. Since Elly is our narrator, it's very clear how much she admired, adored, and supported her brother. Personally, I am, at the conclusion of my reading experience, quite curious as to whether or not Joe was in fact deserving of this level of commitment. Perhaps it's because Joe doesn't always seem to be as real as Elly's childhood friend, Jenny Penny or even the swearing rabbit named "god." <br /><br />Also, Winman oftentimes has Elly mention the world events happening. Some like IRA bombings and the death of John Lennon were clear as to how they impact a young British woman in the years from 68- post 9/11. But other times I felt it didn't really work and these events were just drops to situate readers in the passing years. <br /><br /><br />Overall, I am glad to have taken the time to explore this debut novel.<br /><br />Goodreads Review published 19/05/19</span>
  <a data-text-id="8471209014853123420" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1429957613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1429957613" />
    <a title="Emer (A Little Haze)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4629647-emer-a-little-haze"><img alt="Emer (A Little Haze)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589390599p2/4629647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1429957613?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emer (A Little Haze)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emer (A Little Haze)" href="/user/show/4629647-emer-a-little-haze">Emer (A Little Haze)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4629647-emer-a-little-haze?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4629647-emer-a-little-haze?shelf=read2016">read2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4629647-emer-a-little-haze?shelf=adult-fiction">adult-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4629647-emer-a-little-haze?shelf=3stars">3stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1429957613&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1429957613" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16601317731428966239">‘When God Was a Rabbit’ is a book about love and friendship. It tells the sibling love story of a brother and sister as they go from childhood to adulthood while also being an interesting dissection of family life as seen through the eyes of the youngest family member. The book is told through the point of view of Elly: initially as a young girl who sees the world with magical eyes and then as a grown woman who has become weary of spirit. <br/><br/>The book is split into two halves; quite simply called pa</span>
  <span id="freeText16601317731428966239" style="display:none">‘When God Was a Rabbit’ is a book about love and friendship. It tells the sibling love story of a brother and sister as they go from childhood to adulthood while also being an interesting dissection of family life as seen through the eyes of the youngest family member. The book is told through the point of view of Elly: initially as a young girl who sees the world with magical eyes and then as a grown woman who has become weary of spirit. <br /><br />The book is split into two halves; quite simply called part one and part two. <br /><br />Part one starts with the birth of Elly in 1968 and continues up until December 1980. <br /><br />From the earliest age Elly is portrayed as highly inquisitive and extremely capable. She seemed to have an almost ethereal or other-worldly feel about her and quite happily made conversation and friendships with adults; one such pivotal ‘friendship’ was that with her elderly next door neighbour Mr Golan who turned out to be a very mentally disturbed individual. Within the first few chapters we see the inappropriateness of this relationship with the young girl and something happens, a secret is shared, that shapes the rest of both Elly and her brother Joe’s lives. It is at this point in the book we are introduced to the character God from the story’s title. God is a pet rabbit that Elly’s brother gifts to her so that she will have a more appropriate friend and will not be lonely. A recurring theme throughout this book is Elly’s search for a real god or a better understanding of religion. <br /><br /><br />The most focused upon relationship in this book is the one between Elly and her brother Joe. Joe is five years older than Elly and very protective of her. From her earliest age Elly viewed Joe as “an exotic creature”. He was a rank outsider who liked to quietly rebel against the conservative world. Joe was struggling with being gay in a time period when it was much more difficult to be so. Elly's love for her brother is absolutely beautiful and incredibly sensitively written. <br /><br /><br />When events jumped forward fifteen years for part two of the novel I felt a little let down and I am truly honest, I almost wish they hadn’t. Part one was so magical and the language was so lyrical that I just got lost in the pages of Elly’s childhood and seeing the children as adults somewhat tarnished that happy experience for me. As I was so very anxious about reading the second half of the book perhaps my fear did colour how I responded to the second half. <br /><br />When we met Elly again it was 1995 and she was twenty-seven years old, running from job to job, having panic attacks and had not been in a stable and healthy relationship but did not seem to know why.<br /><br />Her brother Joe had run away to New York where he could feel freer and be more comfortably out. He still seemed to be burdened within his soul whether by a lost childhood love or by the secret that Elly had sworn him to keep and seemed to constantly be hiding from something. <br /><i>
  <blockquote><br />“You had to translate his actions, for they were seldom accompanied by words, because his world was a quiet world; a disconnected, fractured space; a puzzle that made him phone me at 3 o'clock in the morning, asking me for the last piece of the border so he could fill in the sky”</blockquote>
</i><br /><br />A sense of knowing who you are and becoming comfortable with yourself was a great theme of this novel. It was interesting to see how their childhoods had created such complex and troubled adult characters but for me part two was not as moving as part one. It lost some of that feeling of magic; that ethereal touch that the author had so lovingly given to part one. <br /><br /><br />However the plot was carried forward nicely and I sped through this section reading it twice as quickly as the first. The first section felt very languid; reading it reminded me of an afternoon of a hot summer’s day running into a warm night, everything was just so still and calm.<br /><br /><br />I liked how certain political and cultural events were referenced in the book as helpful reminders of the time period e.g. John Lennon's shooting, the London docklands bombing, Princess Diana's funeral, 9/11 etc. It was also very interesting to see how these world events impacted on one small family.<br /><br /><br />I really enjoyed this book. It had a wonderful other worldly quality to it and it was a heartfelt account of the trials and tribulations of a family. It really demonstrated how a person’s whole world really just revolves around a very small number of people: those who you are related to and your closest of friends. Perhaps as a negative you could say this family experienced one or two too many shocks, tragic events or catastrophic life events…it is like the whole gamut of bad experiences are put into this novel, from sexual abuse to hostage taking to amnesia, and sometimes it seemed as if these events were only put in place to shock. However, to give the author their due, each was used as a wonderful plot device and I did enjoy the journey of life that Elly and her family took within the pages of this book. <br /><br /><br />I would recommend this book to anyone who loves reading beautiful, descriptive prose.<br /><br /><br /><b>Three stars</b><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16601317731428966239" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_193398160" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/193398160" />
    <a title="JChipol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5942921-jchipol"><img alt="JChipol" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/193398160?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JChipol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JChipol" href="/user/show/5942921-jchipol">JChipol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/10742682-when-god-was-a-rabbit">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer193398160" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17891727151975242339">Elly and Joe are "two halves of a whole"; sister and brother. Their shared secrets and longing for acceptance & love binds them together tightly and inextricably. Both have friends that change their lives along the way; including both Jenny Penny, Elly’s childhood friend, and Charlie, Joe’s first love. Both must learn to overcome their losses as well, but Elly knows it’s their relationship and companionship that holds them steady. <br/>When God Was a Rabbit is a story of childhood and growing up; fri</span>
  <span id="freeText17891727151975242339" style="display:none">Elly and Joe are "two halves of a whole"; sister and brother. Their shared secrets and longing for acceptance &amp; love binds them together tightly and inextricably. Both have friends that change their lives along the way; including both Jenny Penny, Elly’s childhood friend, and Charlie, Joe’s first love. Both must learn to overcome their losses as well, but Elly knows it’s their relationship and companionship that holds them steady. <br />When God Was a Rabbit is a story of childhood and growing up; friendships and families, the loss of innocence, triumph and tragedy and everything in between.<br />Joe is the one who gives Elly her rabbit, which she calls God (much to her teacher's horror) and Joe is the one who helps her through the difficult things in her life. He keeps her secrets safely tucked away. He is her "love interest" in a purely wholesome and normal way. <br />There were some very funny parts to the book, and it started off quite promising. I especially loved the Nativity fiasco in which Elly's friend Jenny is adamant to play the part of an octopus and Elly changes the bible story to enhance her own part in the play; a debarcle which ultimately causes the resignation of a teacher and the death of baby Jesus. This part of the story made me laugh out loud, but maybe that’s more a reflection of my Catholic school experiences rather than excellence in writing. Even so, I genuinely enjoyed the first few chapters, but, sadly the book doesn’t live up to its early promise.<br />This book was highly recommended to me but though I thought it was ok, I can’t say that I enjoyed it as much as I was expecting; I loved the title, I loved the concept, but the book was in the end quite disappointing. It was as if Sarah Winman was trying to put too many concepts all in one book (9/11, the death of Princess Di, Homosexual, platonic and family love, as well as child abuse, love lost, sexual awakenings, cancer, death and post traumatic amnesia) Every theme was a big one and each deserved more exploration; it all seemed a little too clumsy and, as a result, nothing was explored in enough depth.<br />I would say this book is diverting and probably worth a read if you don’t want anything too taxing. A good beach read, and worth a look, but just not my thing maybe.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17891727151975242339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2370170184" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2370170184" />
    <a title="Dov Zeller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/440651-dov-zeller"><img alt="Dov Zeller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478090196p2/440651.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2370170184?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dov Zeller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dov Zeller" href="/user/show/440651-dov-zeller">Dov Zeller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10806067-when-god-was-a-rabbit">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=sibling-stories">sibling-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=new-york">new-york</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=queer-lgbt">queer-lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=existential-pain-and-angst">existential-pain-and-angst</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=trauma">trauma</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=fiction-21st-cen">fiction-21st-cen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=london">london</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=physical-and-or-emotional-abuse">physical-and-or-emotional-abuse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440651-dov-zeller?shelf=memory">memory</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2370170184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17986550456055576920">How do I review this book, which is so many things at once? Which is in some ways like a soap opera, perhaps just a touch in the tongue and cheek style of Jane the Virgin. And in some ways, the opposite of a soap opera. Drama is in here, but not exploited in a particularly soapy way. In fact, it is often more "background noise". Part of the fabric of life, but certainly not the whole tapestry. Here I am echoing a segment of Tzipora's Goodreads review in which she talks about the narrator's child</span>
  <span id="freeText17986550456055576920" style="display:none">How do I review this book, which is so many things at once? Which is in some ways like a soap opera, perhaps just a touch in the tongue and cheek style of Jane the Virgin. And in some ways, the opposite of a soap opera. Drama is in here, but not exploited in a particularly soapy way. In fact, it is often more "background noise". Part of the fabric of life, but certainly not the whole tapestry. Here I am echoing a segment of Tzipora's Goodreads review in which she talks about the narrator's childhood experience of abuse:<br /><br />"As a whole so much this book revolved around the abuse but it was more a backdrop. And it was so effective and so believable. It reminded me very much of what real life is like when you've been abused. it's there. It effects your life but there is SO MUCH MORE in life than that."<br /><br />So, there is much drama in here, and yet, it is muted in a way. What matters in this book are the relationships between characters, their interdependence. Elly is the narrator, but in some fundamental ways we only get to know her through the other characters in this book.<br /><br />And it is a book about the interdependency of memory itself. How we build our networks of memory with other people, how we need their stories to converge in certain ways with our own, or we think we do. How memories can be vine-like, entangling and entangled. In this novel, the interdependency is part of what makes life rich and worth living, but it can also be harmful--enabling the characters to avoid intimacy, to stick to believing certain stories about themselves that don't allow for change and growth. Shared memory in this novel is a source of self-understanding, a fundamental part of the development of intimacy and 'personal growth'. But this book seems to pose the question: when is shared memory something that allows us to develop a connection with ourselves and with others, and when is it something that draws us to hide from ourselves and from intimacy?<br /><br />Loss (in the form of anguish, trauma, and despair) in this book becomes multi-layered, something that can and can't be shared, something isolating, that may allow for or even intensify communal connection, but at a cost. Winman offers varying scenarios in which trauma is shared openly, and hidden. Elly shares enough about being abused with her brother that they become bonded by it, but it is not necessarily a healthy bond. Perhaps if she had told her parents that would have shifted the dynamic. Meanwhile, Jenny Penny doesn't share openly what is happening in her home life, and instead there is a kind of erratic-ness to her behavior, so much that Elly can't understand. Even a cruelty, a protective and--though understandable--destructive dishonesty. <br /><br />Self-absorption and communal absorption come together. People know each other deeply and don't seem to know each other at all. It's not always clear when relationships are truly mutual, and when their seeming mutuality is just something needed, gone through the motions of, a kind of needful architecture to walk with a certain eerie detachment through the days. The book is far from perfect, but there is an honesty in this portrayal of relationships that, in moments, is as painful as it is stunning. <br /><br />Memory in the world of this novel, reveals itself to be a communal act. Or, there is a vivid tension between communal and individual memory. But in this book there aren't really individuals per se--in a really refreshing way this book sees interdependency as a fact of selfhood, and so, selfhood itself is complexly intertwined with others lives and memories. I find all of this meaningful and intriguing--the novel is one in which a range relationships really matter--several relationships are central to the narrator's experience. Elly's relationship with her parents; her father's sister Nancy, who is also, in some sense, her mother's lover; her brother Joe (a name, that for some reason, never felt quite right, never seemed to fit him); Joe's friend Charlie; Elly's childhood friend Jenny Penny (in the book Jenny Penny has a kind of pen name, and I thought I was about to discover that Jenny Penny WAS this made up name, but no, in the book it's her 'actual' name and every time I read it I think of Miss Moneypenny from James Bond); Arthur, a tenant at their house/inn, a retired academic who is in his fifties or sixties in the first half of the book, and in the second half of it in his seventies and eighties, writing his memoirs which Elly is editing; Arthur's best friend Ginger, as flamboyant as Arthur can, in moments, be, though with Arthur it seems more of a form of revealing himself and with Ginger the flamboyance appears to be something to hide behind. Though, once again, there is tension between the two ideas, the constant tension in this book--when are we showing ourselves to others and when are we hiding? When are we truly seeing others, and when are we grasping for something in them and shaping it in a way that is needful to our own narratives of self-understanding? <br /><br />I am still trying to make sense of the prologue, which has the feel of magical realism to it. <br /><br />I am wondering, what kind of world is this that Winman has built? Why, she made the choices made--the ones that move this in the direction of "realism", and the ones that move it toward the fabular, or magical, or operatic. <br /><br />In a Telegraph article by Tim Auld he says of John Irving's work: "...[W]ith its fantastical flourishes and fateful coincidences, has always been a mere notch away from magical realism." That might be the most accurate way for me to describe Winman's work here. <br /><br />This isn't a perfect book, there are a number of things I could critique in here, but for the moment I'd rather focus on what I did like. The novel is engaging, the narrative voice tender and yet detached in a way that makes sense given what I understand of Elly's character. I love that Elly and her close knit circle are flawed but complex, loving people, that queerness is just a given part of the world of this book, that relationships aren't perfect, but they are what matters most. There is a lot of deeply meaningful exploration in here. I'm glad I read it and look forward to reading more by the author. <br /><br />Here is a review I liked by Goodreads reviewer Blair.<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/170182192">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>?</span>
  <a data-text-id="17986550456055576920" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer9845656295987080649">2.5 stars<br/><br/>Really liked the setting and the focus on childhood and family. Loved pretty much all the secondary characters, they were all quirky and unique, but not in an obnoxious way. Didn't like Elly so much, she was a weird, needy, pathetic main character who was obsessed with her older brother. Also, the second half threw in way too many issues, the whole amnesia, 9/11, cancer and prison thing, and most of it wasn't handled very well.</span>

<div id="review_227611462" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/227611462" />
    <a title="jhldjfau" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4982440-jhldjfau"><img alt="jhldjfau" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/images/nophoto/user/c_111x148.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/227611462?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jhldjfau" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jhldjfau" href="/user/show/4982440-jhldjfau">jhldjfau</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4982440-jhldjfau?shelf=i-own-it">i-own-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4982440-jhldjfau?shelf=great-book-to-gift">great-book-to-gift</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4982440-jhldjfau?shelf=books-that-halt-time">books-that-halt-time</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer227611462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7747597827491365852">This is a beautiful book. I haven't laughed out loud so often, and with such genuine pleasure, since last year's Comedy Festival. And that's saying something, because this book isn't comedy at all; in fact, much of it is tragic and heartbreaking. But this is what makes 'When God was a Rabbit' unique; a book to treasure. It takes the most difficult situations (child neglect, abuse, first love, loneliness, anxiety, separation and loss) and without making light of any of them, deftly skews the pict</span>
  <span id="freeText7747597827491365852" style="display:none">This is a beautiful book. I haven't laughed out loud so often, and with such genuine pleasure, since last year's Comedy Festival. And that's saying something, because this book isn't comedy at all; in fact, much of it is tragic and heartbreaking. But this is what makes 'When God was a Rabbit' unique; a book to treasure. It takes the most difficult situations (child neglect, abuse, first love, loneliness, anxiety, separation and loss) and without making light of any of them, deftly skews the picture so that what is illuminated is not so much the tragedy itself, but the human errors along the way, common enough to all of us as to be both instantly recognisable and... well, tragically funny. It would be too cliched to merely call this 'black humour' - Winman has achieved much more than that. I'm left with the feeling that maybe all family histories, no matter how bleak, bland or banal, are worthy of being told - so long as you can just find the right voice. I think of how many books I read each year that deal with grief, triumph over adversity, "epic sagas" and so on, and I invariably take much away from them. But I don't always enjoy the process. When God Was a Rabbit offers a story that will resonate long after you forget the details, but it's also an absolute pleasure of an experience. <br /><br />I only have one gripe with this book, and that is that I've bought three copies so far and none has stayed resident on my shelf for longer than a week. Admittedly, I keep giving it away, but still - that's a fair warning to any of you who may unwittingly fall into the same trap after reading this book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7747597827491365852" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('J7koayISEaIRj2VhjyBCNrhyIB1Jo1BuQznj02aZlrjFQKRGR5e4Zx4NTbo+k4ZHfv3rcZbF7YLVetkDn/Ec0Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IJn5xEz85zNBO23xNHGAvQLPK8BQv3c/6zPxW7ezDY7CYHXpKXlO9k65RSqFwkTMxEDgrI/ZytN9cMuLTtuH5w==')})
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer1905405831687902467">Sarah Winman (born 1964) is a British actress and author. In 2011 her debut novel When God Was a Rabbit became an international bestseller and won Winman several awards including New Writer of the Year in the Galaxy National Book Awards.</span>
